.navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .navbox .navbar{display:block;font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .navbox-title .navbar{float:left;text-align:left;margin-right:0.5em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Labor" title="Template:Labor"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Labor" title="Template talk:Labor"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Labor&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>A <b>trade union</b> (or a <b>labor union</b> in <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">American English</a>), often simply called a <b>union</b>, is an organization of workers who have come together to achieve many common goals, such as protecting the <a href="/wiki/Integrity" title="Integrity">integrity</a> of their trade, improving safety standards, and attaining better wages, benefits (such as vacation, health care, and retirement), and working conditions through the increased bargaining power wielded by the creation of a monopoly of the workers. Trade unions typically fund the formal organization, head office, and legal team functions of the trade union through regular fees or union dues. The delegate staff of the trade union representation in the workforce are made up of workplace volunteers who are appointed by members in democratic elections.\n</p><p>The trade union, through an elected leadership and bargaining committee, bargains with the employer on behalf of union members (<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/rank_and_file" class="extiw" title="wikt:rank and file">rank and file</a> members) and negotiates <a href="/wiki/Labour_and_employment_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour and employment law">labour contracts</a> (collective bargaining) with employers. The most common purpose of these associations or unions is "maintaining or improving the conditions of their <a href="/wiki/Employment" title="Employment">employment</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-webb_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webb-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> This may include the negotiation of <a href="/wiki/Wage" title="Wage">wages</a>, work rules, occupational health and safety standards, complaint procedures, rules governing status of employees including promotions, just cause conditions for termination, and employment benefits.\n</p><p>Unions may organize a particular section of skilled workers (<a href="/wiki/Craft_unionism" title="Craft unionism">craft unionism</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-Poole,_M._1986_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poole,_M._1986-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> a cross-section of workers from various trades (<a href="/wiki/General_union" title="General union">general unionism</a>), or attempt to organize all workers within a particular industry (<a href="/wiki/Industrial_unionism" title="Industrial unionism">industrial unionism</a>). The agreements negotiated by a union are binding on the rank and file members and the employer and in some cases on other non-member workers. Trade unions traditionally have a constitution which details the governance of their bargaining unit and also have governance at various levels of government depending on the industry that binds them legally to their negotiations and functioning.\n</p><p>Originating in Great Britain, trade unions became popular in many countries during the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. Trade unions may be composed of individual workers, <a href="/wiki/Professional" title="Professional">professionals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Retiree" class="mw-redirect" title="Retiree">past workers</a>, students, <a href="/wiki/Apprentice" class="mw-redirect" title="Apprentice">apprentices</a> or the unemployed. Trade union density, or the percentage of workers belonging to a trade union, <a href="#Prevalence_worldwide">is highest in the Nordic countries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ILO_Density_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILO_Density-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Definition"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Definition</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#National_general_unions"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">National general unions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Legalization_and_expansion"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Legalization and expansion</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Prevalence_worldwide"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Prevalence worldwide</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#Trade_unions_by_country"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Trade unions by country</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li <span class="toctext">Belgium</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Canada"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Canada</span></a></li>\n<li class="toctext">Germany</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#India"><span class="tocnumber">4.8</span> <span class="toctext">India</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 class="tocnumber">4.10</span> <span class="toctext">Mexico</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Nordic_countries"><span class="tocnumber">4.11</span> <span class="toctext">Nordic countries</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Spain"><span class="tocnumber">4.12</span> <span class="toctext">Spain</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#United_Kingdom"><span class="tocnumber">4.13</span> <span class="toctext">United Kingdom</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#United_States"><span class="tocnumber">4.14</span> <span class="toctext">United States</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Vatican_(Holy_See)"><span class="tocnumber">4.15</span> <span class="toctext">Vatican (Holy See)</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Structure_and_politics"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Structure and politics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Shop_types"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Shop types</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Diversity_of_international_unions"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Diversity of international unions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#International_unionization"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">International unionization</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Impact"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Impact</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Economics"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Economics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Politics"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Politics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Health"><span class="tocnumber">9.3</span> <span class="toctext">Health</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Union_publications"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Union publications</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#Film"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Film</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Notes_and_references"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Notes and references</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-35"><a href="#Britain"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Britain</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-36"><a href="#United_States_2"><span class="tocnumber">14.2</span> <span class="toctext">United States</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Other"><span class="tocnumber">14.3</span> <span class="toctext">Other</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Australia_2"><span class="tocnumber">15.1</span> <span class="toctext">Australia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Europe"><span class="tocnumber">15.2</span> <span class="toctext">Europe</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41"><a href="#United_States_3"><span class="tocnumber">15.3</span> <span class="toctext">United States</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-42"><a href="#Jewish_Law_(Halakhah)"><span class="tocnumber">15.4</span> <span class="toctext">Jewish Law (Halakhah)</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Garment_Workers_on_Strike,_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Garment_Workers_on_Strike%2C_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg/250px-Garment_Workers_on_Strike%2C_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Garment_Workers_on_Strike%2C_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg/375px-Garment_Workers_on_Strike%2C_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Garment_Workers_on_Strike%2C_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg/500px-Garment_Workers_on_Strike%2C_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="460" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Garment_Workers_on_Strike,_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Garment workers on strike, New York City circa 1913.</div></div></div>\n<p>Since the publication of the <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Trade_Unionism" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Trade Unionism">History of Trade Unionism</a></i> (1894) by <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Webb" class="mw-redirect" title="Sidney Webb">Sidney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beatrice_Webb" title="Beatrice Webb">Beatrice Webb</a>, the predominant historical view is that a trade union "is a continuous association on wage earners for the purpose of maintaining or improving the conditions of their employment."<sup id="cite_ref-webb_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webb-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> described trade unions thus: "The value of labour-power constitutes the conscious and explicit foundation of the trade unions, whose importance for the [...] working class can scarcely be overestimated. The trade unions aim at nothing less than to prevent the reduction of wages below the level that is traditionally maintained in the various branches of industry. That is to say, they wish to prevent the price of labour-power from falling below its value" (<i>Capital</i> V1, 1867, p.&#160;1069).\n</p><p>A modern definition by the Australian Bureau of Statistics states that a trade union is "an organization consisting predominantly of employees, the principal activities of which include the negotiation of rates of pay and conditions of employment for its members."<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Yet historian R.A. Leeson, in <i>United we Stand</i> (1971), said:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Two conflicting views of the trade-union movement strove for ascendancy in the nineteenth century: one the defensive-restrictive guild-craft tradition passed down through journeymen\'s clubs and friendly societies, ... the other the aggressive-expansionist drive to unite all \'labouring men and women\' for a \'different order of things\'.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Recent historical research by Bob James in <i>Craft, Trade or Mystery</i> (2001) puts forward the view that trade unions are part of a broader movement of <a href="/wiki/Benefit_society" title="Benefit society">benefit societies</a>, which includes medieval guilds, <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemasons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Independent_Order_of_Odd_Fellows" title="Independent Order of Odd Fellows">Oddfellows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Friendly_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="Friendly Society">friendly societies</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Fraternal_organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Fraternal organisation">fraternal organizations</a>.\n</p><p>The 18th century <a href="/wiki/Economist" title="Economist">economist</a> <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> noted the imbalance in the rights of workers in regards to owners (or "masters"). In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wealth_of_Nations" title="The Wealth of Nations">The Wealth of Nations</a></i>, <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Chapter_8:_Of_the_Wages_of_Labour" class="extiw" title="s:Chapter 8: Of the Wages of Labour">Book I, chapter 8</a>, Smith wrote:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We rarely hear, it has been said, of the combination of masters, though frequently of those of workmen. But whoever imagines, upon this account, that masters rarely combine, is as ignorant of the world as of the subject. Masters are always and everywhere in a sort of tacit, but constant and uniform combination, not to raise the wages of labor above their actual rate[.]\nWhen workers combine, masters ... never cease to call aloud for the assistance of the civil magistrate, and the rigorous execution of those laws which have been enacted with so much severity against the combination of servants, labourers and journeymen.</p></blockquote>\n<p>As Smith noted, unions were illegal for many years in most countries, although Smith argued that it should remain illegal to fix wages or prices by employees or employers. There were severe penalties for attempting to organize unions, up to and including execution. Despite this, unions were formed and began to acquire <a href="/wiki/Political_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Political power">political power</a>, eventually resulting in a body of labour law that not only legalized organizing efforts, but codified the relationship between employers and those employees organized into unions.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FrameBreaking-1812.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/FrameBreaking-1812.jpg/220px-FrameBreaking-1812.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/FrameBreaking-1812.jpg/330px-FrameBreaking-1812.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/FrameBreaking-1812.jpg/440px-FrameBreaking-1812.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1045" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FrameBreaking-1812.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early 19th century workplace militancy manifested in the <a href="/wiki/Luddism" class="mw-redirect" title="Luddism">Luddite riots</a>, when unemployed workers destroyed labour saving machines</div></div></div>\n<p>The origins of trade unions can be traced back to 18th century Britain, where the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">rapid expansion of industrial society</a> then taking place drew women, children, rural workers and immigrants into the work force in large numbers and in new roles. They encountered a large hostility in their early existence from employers and government groups; at the time, unions and unionists were regularly prosecuted under various restraint of trade and conspiracy statutes. This pool of unskilled and semi-skilled labour spontaneously organized in fits and starts throughout its beginnings,<sup id="cite_ref-webb_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webb-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> and would later be an important arena for the development of trade unions. Trade unions have sometimes been seen as successors to the <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a> of <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval Europe">medieval Europe</a>, though the relationship between the two is disputed, as the masters of the guilds employed workers (apprentices and journeymen) who were not allowed to organize.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Trade unions and collective bargaining were outlawed from no later than the middle of the 14th century, when the <a href="/wiki/Ordinance_of_Labourers" class="mw-redirect" title="Ordinance of Labourers">Ordinance of Labourers</a> was enacted in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a>, but their way of thinking was the one that endured down the centuries, inspiring evolutions and advances in thinking which eventually gave workers their necessary rights. As collective bargaining and early worker unions grew with the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, the government began to clamp down on what it saw as the danger of popular unrest at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>. In 1799, the <a href="/wiki/Combination_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Combination Act">Combination Act</a> was passed, which banned trade unions and collective bargaining by British workers. Although the unions were subject to often severe repression until 1824, they were already widespread in cities such as <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>. Workplace militancy had also manifested itself as <a href="/wiki/Luddism" class="mw-redirect" title="Luddism">Luddism</a> and had been prominent in struggles such as the <a href="/wiki/1820_Rising" class="mw-redirect" title="1820 Rising">1820 Rising</a> in Scotland, in which 60,000 workers went on a <a href="/wiki/General_strike" title="General strike">general strike</a>, which was soon crushed. Sympathy for the plight of the workers brought repeal of the acts in 1824, although the <a href="/wiki/Combination_Act_1825" class="mw-redirect" title="Combination Act 1825">Combination Act 1825</a> severely restricted their activity.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>By the 1810s, the first labour organizations to bring together workers of divergent occupations were formed. Possibly the first such union was the General Union of Trades, also known as the Philanthropic Society, founded in 1818 in <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>. The latter name was to hide the organization\'s real purpose in a time when trade unions were still illegal.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_general_unions">National general unions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: National general unions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg/170px-London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg/255px-London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg/340px-London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg 2x" data-file-width="808" data-file-height="1272" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poster issued by the London Trades Council, advertising a demonstration held on 2 June 1873</div></div></div>\n<p>The first attempts at setting up a national <a href="/wiki/General_union" title="General union">general union</a> were made in the 1820s and 30s. The <a href="/wiki/National_Association_for_the_Protection_of_Labour" title="National Association for the Protection of Labour">National Association for the Protection of Labour</a> was established in 1830 by <a href="/wiki/John_Doherty_(trade_unionist)" title="John Doherty (trade unionist)">John Doherty</a>, after an apparently unsuccessful attempt to create a similar national presence with the National Union of Cotton-spinners. The Association quickly enrolled approximately 150 unions, consisting mostly of textile related unions, but also including mechanics, blacksmiths, and various others. Membership rose to between 10,000 and 20,000 individuals spread across the five counties of <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheshire" title="Cheshire">Cheshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Derbyshire" title="Derbyshire">Derbyshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nottinghamshire" title="Nottinghamshire">Nottinghamshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leicestershire" title="Leicestershire">Leicestershire</a> within a year.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> To establish awareness and legitimacy, the union started the weekly <i>Voice of the People</i> publication, having the declared intention "to unite the productive classes of the community in one common bond of union."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWebbWebb1894122_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWebbWebb1894122-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1834, the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_People" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh People">Welsh</a> <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialist</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Owen" title="Robert Owen">Robert Owen</a> established the <a href="/wiki/Grand_National_Consolidated_Trades_Union" title="Grand National Consolidated Trades Union">Grand National Consolidated Trades Union</a>. The organization attracted a range of socialists from Owenites to revolutionaries and played a part in the protests after the <a href="/wiki/Tolpuddle_Martyrs" title="Tolpuddle Martyrs">Tolpuddle Martyrs</a>\' case, but soon collapsed.\n</p><p>More permanent trade unions were established from the 1850s, better resourced but often less radical. The <a href="/wiki/London_Trades_Council" title="London Trades Council">London Trades Council</a> was founded in 1860, and the <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_Outrages" title="Sheffield Outrages">Sheffield Outrages</a> spurred the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Trades_Union_Congress" title="Trades Union Congress">Trades Union Congress</a> in 1868, the first long-lived <a href="/wiki/National_trade_union_center" title="National trade union center">national trade union center</a>. By this time, the existence and the demands of the trade unions were becoming accepted by <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a> <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle class</a> opinion. In <i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Political_Economy" title="Principles of Political Economy">Principles of Political Economy</a></i> (1871) <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> wrote:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>If it were possible for the working classes, by combining among themselves, to raise or keep up the general rate of wages, it needs hardly be said that this would be a thing not to be punished, but to be welcomed and rejoiced at. Unfortunately the effect is quite beyond attainment by such means. The multitudes who compose the working class are too numerous and too widely scattered to combine at all, much more to combine effectually. If they could do so, they might doubtless succeed in diminishing the hours of labour, and obtaining the same wages for less work. They would also have a limited power of obtaining, by combination, an increase of general wages at the expense of profits.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legalization_and_expansion">Legalization and expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Legalization and expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg/220px-1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg/330px-1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg/440px-1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="496" data-file-height="395" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Trade union demonstrators held at bay by soldiers during the 1912 <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_textile_strike" class="mw-redirect" title="Lawrence textile strike">Lawrence textile strike</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lawrence,_Massachusetts" title="Lawrence, Massachusetts">Lawrence, Massachusetts</a></div></div></div>\n<p>British trade unions were finally legalized in 1872, after a <i><a href="/wiki/Royal_Commission_on_Trade_Unions" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Commission on Trade Unions">Royal Commission on Trade Unions</a></i> in 1867 agreed that the establishment of the organizations was to the advantage of both employers and employees.\n</p><p>This period also saw the growth of trade unions in other industrializing countries, especially the United States, Germany and France.\n</p><p>In the United States, the first effective nationwide labour organization was the <a href="/wiki/Knights_of_Labor" title="Knights of Labor">Knights of Labor</a>, in 1869, which began to grow after 1880. Legalization occurred slowly as a result of a series of court decisions.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Organized_Trades_and_Labor_Unions" title="Federation of Organized Trades and Labor Unions">Federation of Organized Trades and Labor Unions</a> began in 1881 as a federation of different unions that did not directly enrol workers. In 1886, it became known as the <a href="/wiki/American_Federation_of_Labor" title="American Federation of Labor">American Federation of Labor</a> or AFL.\n</p><p>In Germany the <a href="/wiki/Free_Association_of_German_Trade_Unions" title="Free Association of German Trade Unions">Free Association of German Trade Unions</a> was formed in 1897 after the <a href="/wiki/Conservativism" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservativism">conservative</a> <a href="/wiki/Anti-Socialist_Laws" title="Anti-Socialist Laws">Anti-Socialist Laws</a> of Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a> were repealed.\n</p><p>In France, labour organization was illegal until 1884. The Bourse du Travail was founded in 1887 and merged with the F\xc3\xa9d\xc3\xa9ration nationale des syndicats (National Federation of Trade Unions) in 1895 to form the <a href="/wiki/General_Confederation_of_Labour_(France)" title="General Confederation of Labour (France)">General Confederation of Labour (France)</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevalence_worldwide">Prevalence worldwide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Prevalence worldwide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The prevalence of unions in various countries can be measured by the concept of "union density", which is expressed as a percentage of the total number of workers in a given location who are trade union members.<sup id="cite_ref-ILO_Stats_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILO_Stats-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Trade union density around the world shows great variation.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Country</th>\n<th>Year</th>\n<th>Density (%)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Albania</td>\n<td>2013</td>\n<td>13.3\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Argentina</td>\n<td>2014</td>\n<td>27.7\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Armenia</td>\n<td>2015</td>\n<td>32.2\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Australia</td>\n<td>2016</td>\n<td>14.5\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Austria</td>\n<td>2016</td>\n<td>26.9\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Belgium</td>\n<td>2018</td>\n<td>65.0\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Belize</td>\n<td>2012</td>\n<td>9.1\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Bermuda</td>\n<td>2012</td>\n<td>23.0\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Bolivia</td>\n<td>2014</td>\n<td>39.1\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Bosnia Kingdom</td>\n<td>2016</td>\n<td>23.5\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>United States</td>\n<td>2016</td>\n<td>10.3\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Vietnam</td>\n<td>2011</td>\n<td>14.6\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Zambia</td>\n<td>2014</td>\n<td>25.9\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Zimbabwe</td>\n<td>2010</td>\n<td>7.5\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Source: ILO<sup id="cite_ref-ILO_Density_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILO_Density-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_unions_by_country">Trade unions by country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Trade unions by country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Australian_labour_movement" title="Australian labour movement">Australian labour movement</a> generally sought to end <a href="/wiki/Child_labour" title="Child labour">child labour</a> practices, improve <a href="/wiki/Worker_safety" class="mw-redirect" title="Worker safety">worker safety</a>, increase wages for both union workers and non-union workers, raise the entire society\'s <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a>, reduce the hours in a work week, provide public education for children, and bring other benefits to <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working class</a> families.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Melbourne_Trades_Hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Melbourne Trades Hall">Melbourne Trades Hall</a> was opened in 1859 with <a href="/wiki/Labour_council" title="Labour council">Trades and Labour Councils</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trades_Hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Trades Hall">Trades Halls</a> opening in all cities and most regional towns in the next forty years. During the 1880s <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions" class="mw-redirect" title="Trade unions">Trade unions</a> developed among <a href="/wiki/Sheep_shearer" title="Sheep shearer">shearers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miner" title="Miner">miners</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stevedore" title="Stevedore">stevedores</a> (wharf workers), but soon spread to cover almost all <a href="/wiki/Blue-collar" class="mw-redirect" title="Blue-collar">blue-collar</a> jobs. Shortages of labour led to high wages for a prosperous skilled working class, whose unions demanded and got an <a href="/wiki/Eight-hour_day" title="Eight-hour day">eight-hour day</a> and other benefits unheard of in Europe.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg/220px-Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg/330px-Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg/440px-Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Eight-hour_day" title="Eight-hour day">Eight-hour day</a> march circa 1900, outside Parliament House in Spring Street, <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Australia gained a reputation as "the working man\'s paradise." Some employers tried to undercut the unions by importing Chinese labour. This produced a reaction which led to all the colonies restricting Chinese and other Asian immigration. This was the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/White_Australia_Policy" class="mw-redirect" title="White Australia Policy">White Australia Policy</a>. The "Australian compact", based around centralised industrial arbitration, a degree of government assistance particularly for primary industries, and White Australia, was to continue for many years before gradually dissolving in the second half of the 20th century.\n</p><p>In the 1870s and 1880s, the growing <a href="/wiki/Australian_labour_movement" title="Australian labour movement">trade union</a> movement began a series of protests against foreign labour. Their arguments were that Asians and Chinese took jobs away from white men, worked for "substandard" wages, lowered working conditions and refused unionisation.<sup id="cite_ref-Markey_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Markey-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Objections to these arguments came largely from wealthy land owners in rural areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Markey_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Markey-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> It was argued that without Asiatics to work in the tropical areas of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Territory" title="Northern Territory">Northern Territory</a> and Queensland, the area would have to be abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-Griffiths_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Griffiths-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Despite these objections to restricting immigration, between 1875 and 1888 all Australian colonies enacted legislation which excluded all further Chinese immigration.<sup id="cite_ref-Griffiths_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Griffiths-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Asian immigrants already residing in the Australian colonies were not expelled and retained the same rights as their Anglo and Southern compatriots.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Barton_Government" title="Barton Government">Barton Government</a> which came to power after the first elections to the Commonwealth parliament in 1901 was formed by the <a href="/wiki/Protectionist_Party" title="Protectionist Party">Protectionist Party</a> with the support of the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Labor_Party" title="Australian Labor Party">Australian Labor Party</a>. The support of the Labor Party was contingent upon restricting non-white immigration, reflecting the attitudes of the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Workers_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Australian Workers Union">Australian Workers Union</a> and other labour organisations at the time, upon whose support the Labor Party was founded.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baltic_states">Baltic states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Baltic states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the Baltic states trade unions were the part of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> trade union system and closely connected with the <a href="/wiki/Party" title="Party">party</a> in the state. <a href="/wiki/Industrial_action" title="Industrial action">Industrial actions</a> were not a part of their activities. After 1990 trade unions in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic states</a> have experienced rapid loss of membership and <a href="/wiki/Economic_power" title="Economic power">economic power</a>, while employers\xe2\x80\x99 organisations increased both power and membership. Low financial and organisational capacity caused by declining membership adds to the problem of interest definition, aggregation and <a href="/wiki/Protection" title="Protection">protection</a> in <a href="/wiki/Negotiations" class="mw-redirect" title="Negotiations">negotiations</a> with employers\xe2\x80\x99 and state organisations. Even the difference exists in the way of organization trade union and density. Starting from 2008 the union density slightly decrease in <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>. In case of <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> this indicator is lower than in <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> but stays stable average 7 percent from total number of <a href="/wiki/Employment" title="Employment">employment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Historical legitimacy is one of the negative factors that determine low associational power.<sup id="cite_ref-pjms.zim.pcz.pl_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pjms.zim.pcz.pl-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belgium">Belgium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Belgium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_trade_unions_in_Belgium" title="List of trade unions in Belgium">List of trade unions in Belgium</a></div>\n<p>With 65% of the workers belonging to a union, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> is a country with one of the highest percentages of trade union membership. Only the Scandinavian countries have a higher trade union density. The biggest union with around 1.7&#160;million members is the Christian democrat <a href="/wiki/Confederation_of_Christian_Trade_Unions" title="Confederation of Christian Trade Unions">Confederation of Christian Trade Unions</a> (ACV-CSC) which was founded in 1904.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The origins of the union can be traced back to the "Anti-Socialist Cotton Workers Union" that was founded in 1886.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The second biggest union is the socialist <a href="/wiki/General_Federation_of_Belgian_Labour" class="mw-redirect" title="General Federation of Belgian Labour">General Federation of Belgian Labour</a> (ABVV-FGTB) which has a membership of more than 1.5&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The ABVV-FGTB traces its origins to 1857, when the first Belgian union was founded in <a href="/wiki/Ghent" title="Ghent">Ghent</a> by a group of <a href="/wiki/Weaving" title="Weaving">weavers</a>. This and other socialist unions became unified around 1898. The ABVV-FGTB in its current form dates back to 1945. The third major multi-sector union in Belgium is the liberal (classical liberal) union <a href="/wiki/General_Confederation_of_Liberal_Trade_Unions_of_Belgium" title="General Confederation of Liberal Trade Unions of Belgium">General Confederation of Liberal Trade Unions of Belgium</a> (ACLVB-CGSLB) which is relatively small in comparison to the first two with a little under 290 thousand members.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The ACLVB-CGSLB was founded in 1920 in an effort to unite the many small liberal unions. Back then the liberal union was known as the "Nationale Centrale der Liberale Vakbonden van Belgi\xc3\xab". In 1930, the ACLVB-CGSLB adopted its current name.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Besides these "big three" there are a number of smaller unions, some more influential than others. These smaller unions tend to specialize in one profession or economic sector. Next to these specialized unions there is also the <a href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutrale_en_Onafhankelijke_Vakbond" class="extiw" title="nl:Neutrale en Onafhankelijke Vakbond">Neutral and Independent Union</a> that rejects the <a href="/wiki/Pillarization" class="mw-redirect" title="Pillarization">pillarization</a> of the "big three" trade unions (their affiliation with political parties). There is also a small <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flemish</a> nationalist union that exists only in the <a href="/wiki/Flemish_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Flemish language">Flemish</a>-speaking part of Belgium, called the <a href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vlaamse_Solidaire_Vakbond" class="extiw" title="nl:Vlaamse Solidaire Vakbond">Vlaamse Solidaire Vakbond</a>. The last Belgian union worth mentioning is the very small, but highly active anarchist union called the <a href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vrije_Bond" class="extiw" title="nl:Vrije Bond">Vrije Bond</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_trade_unions_in_Canada" title="List of trade unions in Canada">List of trade unions in Canada</a></div>\n<p>Canada\'s first trade union, the Labourers\' Benevolent Association (now International Longshoremen\'s Association Local 273), formed in <a href="/wiki/Saint_John,_New_Brunswick" title="Saint John, New Brunswick">Saint John, New Brunswick</a> in 1849. The union was formed when Saint John\'s longshoremen banded together to lobby for regular pay and a shorter workday.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Canadian unionism had early ties with <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Britain</a> and Ireland. Tradesmen who came from Britain brought traditions of the British trade union movement, and many British unions had branches in Canada. Canadian unionism\'s ties with the United States eventually replaced those with Britain.\n</p><p>Collective bargaining was first recognized in 1945, after the strike by the <a href="/wiki/United_Auto_Workers" title="United Auto Workers">United Auto Workers</a> at the <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>\' plant in <a href="/wiki/Oshawa,_Ontario" class="mw-redirect" title="Oshawa, Ontario">Oshawa, Ontario</a>.\nJustice <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Rand" title="Ivan Rand">Ivan Rand</a> issued a landmark legal decision after the strike in <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Ontario" title="Windsor, Ontario">Windsor, Ontario</a>, involving 17,000 <a href="/wiki/Ford" class="mw-redirect" title="Ford">Ford</a> workers. He granted the union the compulsory check-off of union dues. Rand ruled that all workers in a bargaining unit benefit from a union-negotiated contract. Therefore, he reasoned they must pay union dues, although they do not have to join the union.\n</p><p>The post-<a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> era also saw an increased pattern of unionization in the public service. Teachers, nurses, social workers, professors and cultural workers (those employed in museums, orchestras and art galleries) all sought private-sector collective bargaining rights. The <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Labour_Congress" title="Canadian Labour Congress">Canadian Labour Congress</a> was founded in 1956 as the <a href="/wiki/National_trade_union_center" title="National trade union center">national trade union center</a> for Canada.\n</p><p>In the 1970s the federal government came under intense pressures to curtail labour cost and inflation. In 1975, the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_of_Canada" title="Liberal Party of Canada">Liberal</a> government of <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Trudeau" title="Pierre Trudeau">Pierre Trudeau</a> introduced mandatory price and wage controls. Under the new law, wages increases were monitored and those ruled to be unacceptably high were rolled back by the government.\n</p><p>Pressures on unions continued into the 1980s and \'90s. Private sector unions faced plant closures in many manufacturing industries and demands to reduce wages and increase productivity. Public sector unions came under attack by federal and provincial governments as they attempted to reduce spending, reduce taxes and balance budgets. Legislation was introduced in many jurisdictions reversing union collective bargaining rights, and many jobs were lost to contractors.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Prominent domestic unions in Canada include <a href="/wiki/ACTRA" title="ACTRA">ACTRA</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Union_of_Postal_Workers" title="Canadian Union of Postal Workers">Canadian Union of Postal Workers</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Union_of_Public_Employees" title="Canadian Union of Public Employees">Canadian Union of Public Employees</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Public_Service_Alliance_of_Canada" title="Public Service Alliance of Canada">Public Service Alliance of Canada</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Public_and_General_Employees" title="National Union of Public and General Employees">National Union of Public and General Employees</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Unifor" title="Unifor">Unifor</a>. International unions active in Canada include the <a href="/wiki/International_Alliance_of_Theatrical_Stage_Employees" title="International Alliance of Theatrical Stage Employees">International Alliance of Theatrical Stage Employees</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Automobile_Workers" class="mw-redirect" title="United Automobile Workers">United Automobile Workers</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Food_and_Commercial_Workers" title="United Food and Commercial Workers">United Food and Commercial Workers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_Steelworkers" title="United Steelworkers">United Steelworkers</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colombia">Colombia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Colombia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_Colombia" title="Trade unions in Colombia">Trade unions in Colombia</a></div>\n<p>Until around 1990 Colombian trade unions were among the strongest in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-JFA11_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JFA11-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> However, the 1980s expansion of <a href="/wiki/Paramilitarism_in_Colombia" class="mw-redirect" title="Paramilitarism in Colombia">paramilitarism in Colombia</a> saw trade union leaders and members increasingly targeted for assassination, and as a result Colombia has been the most dangerous country in the world for trade unionists for several decades.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Between 2000 and 2010 Colombia accounted for 63.12% of trade unionists murdered globally.<sup id="cite_ref-ITUCres_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITUCres-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Trade_Union_Confederation" title="International Trade Union Confederation">International Trade Union Confederation</a> (ITUC) there were 2832 murders of trade unionists between 1 January 1986 and 30 April 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-ITUCres_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITUCres-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> meaning that "on average, men and women trade unionists in Colombia have been killed at the rate of one every three days over the last 23 years."<sup id="cite_ref-ITUC2010_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITUC2010-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Costa_Rica">Costa Rica</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Costa Rica">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_Costa_Rica" title="Trade unions in Costa Rica">Trade unions in Costa Rica</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Agricultores,_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica,_enero_2011.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Agricultores%2C_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica%2C_enero_2011.jpg/220px-Agricultores%2C_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica%2C_enero_2011.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Agricultores%2C_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica%2C_enero_2011.jpg/330px-Agricultores%2C_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica%2C_enero_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Agricultores%2C_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica%2C_enero_2011.jpg/440px-Agricultores%2C_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica%2C_enero_2011.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Agricultores,_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica,_enero_2011.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Costa Rican agricultural unions demonstration, January 2011</div></div></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>, trade unions first appeared in the late 1800s to support workers in a variety of urban and industrial jobs, such as railroad builders and craft tradesmen.<sup id="cite_ref-SITRAPEQUIA_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SITRAPEQUIA-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> After facing violent repression, such as during the 1934 United Fruit Strike, unions gained more power after the 1948 <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rican_Civil_War" title="Costa Rican Civil War">Costa Rican Civil War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SITRAPEQUIA_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SITRAPEQUIA-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Today, Costa Rican unions are strongest in the public sector, including the fields of education and medicine, but also have a strong presence in the agricultural sector.<sup id="cite_ref-SITRAPEQUIA_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SITRAPEQUIA-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In general, Costa Rican unions support government regulation of the banking, medical, and education fields, as well as improved wages and working conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_Germany" title="Trade unions in Germany">Trade unions in Germany</a></div>\n<p>Trade unions in Germany have a history reaching back to the <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848_in_the_German_states" class="mw-redirect" title="Revolutions of 1848 in the German states">German revolution</a> in 1848, and still play an important role in the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Germany" title="Economy of Germany">German economy</a> and society. In 1875 the SDP, the Social Democratic Party of Germany, which is one of the biggest political parties in Germany, supported the forming of unions in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The most important labour organisation is the <a href="/wiki/German_Confederation_of_Trade_Unions" class="mw-redirect" title="German Confederation of Trade Unions">German Confederation of Trade Unions</a> <i>(Deutscher Gewerkschaftsbund \xe2\x80\x93 DGB)</i>, which represents more than 6&#160;million people (31 December 2011) and is the umbrella association of several single trade unions for special economic sectors. The DGB is not the only Union Organization that represents the working trade. There are smaller organizations, such as the CGB, which is a Christian-based confederation, that represent over 1.5&#160;million people.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_India" title="Trade unions in India">Trade unions in India</a></div>\n<p>In India, the Trade Union movement is generally divided on political lines. According to provisional statistics from the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Labour_and_Employment_(India)" title="Ministry of Labour and Employment (India)">Ministry of Labour</a>, trade unions had a combined membership of 24,601,589 in 2002. As of 2008, there are 12 Central Trade Union Organisations (CTUO) recognized by the Ministry of Labour.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The forming of these unions was a big deal in India. It led to a big push for more regulatory laws which gave workers a lot more power.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/AITUC" class="mw-redirect" title="AITUC">AITUC</a> is the oldest trade union in India. It is a left supported organization. \nA trade union with nearly 2,000,000 members is the Self Employed Women\'s Association (SEWA) which protects the rights of Indian women working in the informal economy. In addition to the protection of rights, SEWA educates, mobilizes, finances, and exalts their members\' trades.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Multiple other organizations represent workers. These organizations are formed upon different political groups. These different groups allow different groups of people with different political views to join a Union.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Labor_unions_in_Japan" title="Labor unions in Japan">Labor unions in Japan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NUGW_May_Day_2011.jpg" class="image"><img alt="NUGW May Day 2011" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/NUGW_May_Day_2011.jpg/220px-NUGW_May_Day_2011.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/NUGW_May_Day_2011.jpg/330px-NUGW_May_Day_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/35/NUGW_May_Day_2011.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="266" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NUGW_May_Day_2011.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>2011 <a href="/wiki/Zenrokyo" class="mw-redirect" title="Zenrokyo">National Trade Union Council (<i>Zenrokyo</i>)</a> <a href="/wiki/International_Workers%27_Day#Japan" title="International Workers&#39; Day">May Day</a> march, Tokyo</div></div></div>\n<p>Trade unions emerged in Japan in the second half of the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Meiji period">Meiji period</a> as the country underwent a period of rapid <a href="/wiki/Industrialization" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrialization">industrialization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nimura_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nimura-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Until 1945, however, the labour movement remained weak, impeded by lack of legal rights,<sup id="cite_ref-Cross_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cross-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anti-union" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-union">anti-union</a> legislation,<sup id="cite_ref-Nimura_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nimura-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> management-organised factory councils, and political divisions between \xe2\x80\x9ccooperative\xe2\x80\x9d and radical unionists.<sup id="cite_ref-Weathers_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weathers-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In the immediate aftermath of the Second World War, the <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Japan" title="Occupation of Japan">US Occupation</a> authorities initially encouraged the formation of independent unions.<sup id="cite_ref-Cross_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cross-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Legislation was passed that enshrined the right to organise,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> and membership rapidly rose to 5&#160;million by February 1947.<sup id="cite_ref-Cross_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cross-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The organisation rate, however, peaked at 55.8% in 1949 and subsequently declined to 18.2% (2006).<sup id="cite_ref-Jil_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jil-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The labour movement went through a process of reorganisation from 1987 to 1991<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> from which emerged the present configuration of three major trade union federations, <a href="/wiki/RENGO" title="RENGO">Rengo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zenroren" title="Zenroren">Zenroren</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zenrokyo" class="mw-redirect" title="Zenrokyo">Zenrokyo</a>, along with other smaller national union organisations.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexico">Mexico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Mexico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Before the 1990s, unions in Mexico had been historically part of a state institutional system. From 1940 until the 1980s, during the worldwide spread of <a href="/wiki/Neoliberalism" title="Neoliberalism">neoliberalism</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Consensus" title="Washington Consensus">Washington Consensus</a>, the Mexican unions did not operate independently, but instead as part of a state institutional system, largely controlled by the ruling party.<sup id="cite_ref-BotzJay01052010_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BotzJay01052010-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During these 40 years, the primary aim of the trade unions was not to benefit the workers, but to carry out the state\'s economic policy under their cosy relationship with the ruling party. This economic policy, which peaked in the 1950s and 60s with the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Mexican_Miracle" class="mw-redirect" title="Mexican Miracle">Mexican Miracle</a>", saw rising incomes and improved standards of living but the primary beneficiaries were the wealthy.<sup id="cite_ref-BotzJay01052010_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BotzJay01052010-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1980s, Mexico began adhering to Washington Consensus policies, selling off state industries such as railroad and telecommunications to private industries. The new owners had an antagonistic attitude towards unions, which, accustomed to comfortable relationships with the state, were not prepared to fight back. A movement of new unions began to emerge under a more independent model, while the former institutionalized unions had become very corrupt, violent, and led by gangsters. From the 1990s onwards, this new model of independent unions prevailed, a number of them represented by the National Union of Workers / Uni\xc3\xb3n Nacional de Trabajadores.<sup id="cite_ref-BotzJay01052010_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BotzJay01052010-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Current old institutions like the Oil Workers Union and the <a href="/wiki/National_Education_Workers%27_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="National Education Workers&#39; Union">National Education Workers\' Union</a> (<i>Sindicato Nacional de Trabajadores de la Educaci\xc3\xb3n</i>, or SNTE) are examples of how the use of government benefits are not being applied to improve the quality in the investigation of the use of oil or the basic education in Mexico as long as their leaders show publicly that they are living wealthily. With 1.4&#160;million members, the teachers\' union is <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>\'s largest; half of Mexico\'s government employees are teachers. It controls school curriculums, and all teacher appointments. Until recently, retiring teachers routinely "gave" their lifelong appointment to a relative or "sell" it for anywhere in between $4,700 and $11,800.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nordic_countries">Nordic countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Nordic countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG/220px-Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG/330px-Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG/440px-Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Workers on strike in Oslo, Norway, 2012</div></div></div>\n<p>Trade unions (Danish: <i>Fagforeninger</i>, Norwegian: <i>Fagforeninger/Fagforeiningar</i> Swedish: <i>Fackf\xc3\xb6reningar</i>) have a long tradition in <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic</a> society. Beginning in the mid-19th century, they today have a large impact on the nature of employment and workers\' rights in many of the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic countries</a>. One of the largest trade unions in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Confederation_of_Trade_Unions" class="mw-redirect" title="Swedish Confederation of Trade Unions">Swedish Confederation of Trade Unions</a>, (LO, <i>Landsorganisationen</i>), incorporating unions such as the Swedish Metal Workers\' Union (<i>IF Metall</i> = <i>Industrifacket Metall</i>), the Swedish Electricians\' Union (Svenska Elektrikerf\xc3\xb6rbundet) and the Swedish Municipality Workers\' Union (<i>Svenska Kommunalarbetaref\xc3\xb6rbundet</i>, abbreviated <i>Kommunal</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> One of the aims of IF Metall is to transform jobs into "good jobs", also called "developing jobs".<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Swedish system is strongly based on the so-called Swedish model, which argues the importance of collective agreements between trade unions and employers.<sup id="cite_ref-pjms.zim.pcz.pl_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pjms.zim.pcz.pl-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Today, the world\'s highest rates of union membership are in the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic countries</a>. As of 2018 or latest year, the percentage of workers belonging to a union (trade union density) was 90.4% in <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, 67.2% in <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, 66.1% in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, 64.4 in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> and 52.5% in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, while it is unknown in <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a> and the <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land_Islands" title="\xc3\x85land Islands">\xc3\x85land Islands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Excluding full-time students working part-time, Swedish union density was 68% in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In all the Nordic countries with a <a href="/wiki/Ghent_system" title="Ghent system">Ghent system</a>\xe2\x80\x94Sweden,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Denmark and <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>\xe2\x80\x94union density is about 70%. The considerably raised membership fees of Swedish union unemployment funds implemented by the new center-right government in January 2007 caused large drops in membership in both unemployment funds and trade unions. From 2006 to 2008, union density declined by six percentage points: from 77% to 71%.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spain">Spain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Spain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_trade_unions_in_Spain" title="List of trade unions in Spain">List of trade unions in Spain</a></div>\n<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_civil_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Spanish civil war">Spanish civil war</a> <a href="/wiki/Anarchists" class="mw-redirect" title="Anarchists">anarchists</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Syndicalists" class="mw-redirect" title="Syndicalists">syndicalists</a> took control over much of Spain. Implementing worker control through a system of <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_socialism" title="Libertarian socialism">libertarian socialism</a> with organizations like the <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-syndicalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anarcho-syndicalist">anarcho-syndicalist</a> <a href="/wiki/Confederaci%C3%B3n_Nacional_del_Trabajo" title="Confederaci\xc3\xb3n Nacional del Trabajo">CNT</a> organizing throughout Spain. Unions were particularly present in <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Catalonia" title="Revolutionary Catalonia">Revolutionary Catalonia</a>, in which anarchists were already the basis for most of society with over 90% of industries being organized through work cooperatives.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The republicans, anarchists and leftists would later lose control over Spain, with <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a> becoming dictator of Spain.\n</p><p>During the fascist regime of Spain the Francoist regime saw the worker movement and union movement as a threat, Franco banned all existing trade unions and set up the government controlled <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Syndical_Organization" title="Spanish Syndical Organization">Spanish Syndical Organization</a> as the only legal Spanish trade union, with the organization existing to maintain Francos power.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many anarchists, communists and leftists turned towards insurgent tactics as Franco implemented wide reaching authoritarian policies, with the CNT and other unions being forced underground. Anarchists would operate covertly setting up local organizations and underground movements to challenge Franco.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> On the 20 of December the <a href="/wiki/ETA_(separatist_group)" title="ETA (separatist group)">ETA</a> <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Luis_Carrero_Blanco" title="Assassination of Luis Carrero Blanco">assassinated Luis Carrero</a>. The death of Carrero Blanco had numerous political implications. By the end of 1973, the physical health of Francisco Franco had declined significantly, and it epitomized the final crisis of the Francoist regime. After his death, the most conservative sector of the Francoist State, known as the b\xc3\xbanker, wanted to influence Franco so that he would choose an ultraconservative as Prime Minister. Finally, he chose Carlos Arias Navarro, who originally announced a partial relaxation of the most rigid aspects of the Francoist State, but quickly retreated under pressure from the b\xc3\xbanker. After Franco\'s death Arias Navarro began relaxing Spanish authoritarianism.\n</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_transition_to_democracy" title="Spanish transition to democracy">Spanish transition to democracy</a>, leftist organizations became legal once again. In modern Spain trade unions now contribute massively towards Spanish society, being again the main catalyst for political change in Spain, with cooperatives employing large parts of the Spanish population such as the <a href="/wiki/Mondragon_Corporation" title="Mondragon Corporation">Mondragon Corporation</a>. Trade unions today lead mass protests against the Spanish government, and are one of the main vectors of political change.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Trade unions in the United Kingdom">Trade unions in the United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_trade_unions_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of trade unions in the United Kingdom">History of trade unions in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg/220px-Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg/330px-Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg/440px-Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1067" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Public sector workers in <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a> striking over pension changes by the government in November 2011</div></div></div>\n<p>Moderate <a href="/wiki/New_Model_Union" title="New Model Union">New Model Unions</a> dominated the union movement from the mid-19th century and where trade unionism was stronger than the political labour movement until the formation and growth of the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> in the early years of the 20th century.\n</p><p>Trade unionism in the United Kingdom was a major factor in some of the economic crises during the 1960s and the 1970s, culminating in the "<a href="/wiki/Winter_of_Discontent" title="Winter of Discontent">Winter of Discontent</a>" of late-1978 and early-1979, when a significant percentage of the nation\'s public sector workers went on strike. By this stage, some 12,000,000 workers in the United Kingdom were trade union members. However, the election victory of the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> at the <a href="/wiki/1979_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1979 United Kingdom general election">1979 general election</a>, at the expense of Labour\'s <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a>, saw substantial trade union reform which saw the level of strikes fall. The level of trade union membership also fell sharply in the 1980s, and continued falling for most of the 1990s. The long decline of most of the industries in which manual trade unions were strong&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 e.g. steel, coal, printing, the docks&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 was one of the causes of this loss of trade union members.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2011, there were 6,135,126 members in TUC-affiliated unions, down from a peak of 12,172,508 in 1980.
