Trade union density was 14.1% in the private sector and 56.5% in the public sector.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Labor_unions_in_the_United_States" title="Labor unions in the United States">Labor unions in the United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Labor_history_of_the_United_States" title="Labor history of the United States">Labor history of the United States</a></div>\n<p>Labor unions are legally recognized as representatives of workers in many industries in the United States. In the United States, unions were formed based on power with the people, not over the people like the government at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Their activity today centres on <a href="/wiki/Collective_bargaining" title="Collective bargaining">collective bargaining</a> over wages, benefits and working conditions for their membership, and on representing their members in disputes with management over violations of contract provisions. Larger unions also typically engage in lobbying activities and supporting endorsed candidates at the state and federal level.\n</p><p>Most unions in America are aligned with one of two larger umbrella organizations: the <a href="/wiki/AFL-CIO" class="mw-redirect" title="AFL-CIO">AFL-CIO</a> created in 1955, and the <a href="/wiki/Change_to_Win_Federation" title="Change to Win Federation">Change to Win Federation</a> which split from the AFL-CIO in 2005. Both advocate policies and legislation on behalf of workers in the United States and Canada, and take an active role in politics. The AFL-CIO is especially concerned with global trade issues.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg/220px-Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg/330px-Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg/440px-Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4803" data-file-height="3256" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Child_labour" title="Child labour">Child labourers</a> in an <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> glass works. Labor unions have an objective interest in combating child labour.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 2010, the percentage of workers belonging to a union in the United States (or total labor union "density") was 11.4%, compared to 18.3% in Japan, 27.5% in Canada and 70% in Finland.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Union membership in the private sector has fallen under 7%<sup id="cite_ref-BLS01272012_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BLS01272012-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> \xe2\x80\x93 levels not seen since 1932. Unions allege that employer-incited opposition has contributed to this decline in membership.\n</p><p>The most prominent unions are among <a href="/wiki/Public_sector" title="Public sector">public sector</a> employees such as teachers, police and other non-managerial or non-executive federal, state, county and municipal employees. Members of unions are disproportionately older, male and residents of the Northeast, the Midwest, and California.<sup id="cite_ref-Yeselson_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yeselson-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Union workers in the private sector average 10\xe2\x80\x9330% higher pay than non-union in America after controlling for individual, job, and labour market characteristics.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Because of their inherently governmental function, public sector workers are paid the same regardless of union affiliation or non-affiliation after controlling for individual, job, and labour market characteristics.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Vatican_.28Holy_See.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Vatican_(Holy_See)">Vatican (Holy See)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Vatican (Holy See)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Vatican_Lay_Workers" title="Association of Vatican Lay Workers">Association of Vatican Lay Workers</a> represents lay employees in the Vatican.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_politics">Structure and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Structure and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cesar_chavez2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Cesar_chavez2.jpg/220px-Cesar_chavez2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Cesar_chavez2.jpg/330px-Cesar_chavez2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Cesar_chavez2.jpg/440px-Cesar_chavez2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2778" data-file-height="1827" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cesar_chavez2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cesar_Chavez" title="Cesar Chavez">Cesar Chavez</a> speaking at a 1974 <a href="/wiki/United_Farm_Workers" title="United Farm Workers">United Farm Workers</a> rally in <a href="/wiki/Delano,_California" title="Delano, California">Delano, California</a>. The UFW during Chavez\'s tenure was committed to restricting immigration.</div></div></div>\n<p>Unions may organize a particular section of skilled workers (<a href="/wiki/Craft_unionism" title="Craft unionism">craft unionism</a>, traditionally found in Australia, Canada, Denmark, Norway, Sweden, Switzerland, the UK and the US<sup id="cite_ref-Poole,_M._1986_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poole,_M._1986-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>), a cross-section of workers from various trades (<a href="/wiki/General_union" title="General union">general unionism</a>, traditionally found in Australia, Belgium, Canada, Denmark, Netherlands, the UK and the US), or attempt to organize all workers within a particular industry (<a href="/wiki/Industrial_unionism" title="Industrial unionism">industrial unionism</a>, found in Australia, Canada, Germany, Finland, Norway, South Korea, Sweden, Switzerland, the UK and the US). These unions are often divided into "<a href="/wiki/Local_union" title="Local union">locals</a>", and united in national <a href="/wiki/List_of_federations_of_trade_unions" title="List of federations of trade unions">federations</a>. These federations themselves will affiliate with <a href="/wiki/International_organization" title="International organization">Internationals</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/International_Trade_Union_Confederation" title="International Trade Union Confederation">International Trade Union Confederation</a>. However, in Japan, union organization is slightly different due to the presence of enterprise unions, i.e. unions that are specific to a plant or company. These enterprise unions, however, join industry-wide federations which in turn are members of <a href="/wiki/RENGO" title="RENGO">Rengo</a>, the Japanese national trade union confederation.\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>, professional associations often carry out the functions of a trade union. In these cases, they may be negotiating for white-collar or professional workers, such as physicians, engineers or teachers. Typically such trade unions refrain from politics or pursue a more liberal politics than their blue-collar counterparts.\n</p><p>A union may acquire the status of a "<a href="/wiki/Juristic_person" class="mw-redirect" title="Juristic person">juristic person</a>" (an artificial legal entity), with a mandate to negotiate with employers for the workers it represents. In such cases, unions have certain legal rights, most importantly the right to engage in <a href="/wiki/Collective_bargaining" title="Collective bargaining">collective bargaining</a> with the employer (or employers) over wages, working hours, and other <a href="/wiki/Employment_contract" title="Employment contract">terms and conditions of employment</a>. The inability of the parties to reach an agreement may lead to <a href="/wiki/Industrial_action" title="Industrial action">industrial action</a>, culminating in either <a href="/wiki/Strike_action" title="Strike action">strike action</a> or management <a href="/wiki/Lockout_(industry)" title="Lockout (industry)">lockout</a>, or binding arbitration. In extreme cases, violent or illegal activities may develop around these events.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg/220px-Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg/330px-Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg/440px-Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1449" data-file-height="930" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Southwest_Railroad_Strike_of_1886" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Southwest Railroad Strike of 1886">Great Southwest Railroad Strike of 1886</a> was a trade union strike involving more than 200,000 workers<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In other circumstances, unions may not have the legal right to represent workers, or the right may be in question. This lack of status can range from non-recognition of a union to political or criminal prosecution of union activists and members, with many cases of violence and deaths having been recorded historically.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unions may also engage in broader political or social struggle. <a href="/wiki/Social_Movement_Unionism" class="mw-redirect" title="Social Movement Unionism">Social Unionism</a> encompasses many unions that use their organizational strength to advocate for social policies and legislation favourable to their members or to workers in general. As well, unions in some countries are closely aligned with <a href="/wiki/Political_party" title="Political party">political parties</a>.\n</p><p>Unions are also delineated by the <a href="/wiki/Service_model" title="Service model">service model</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Organizing_model" title="Organizing model">organizing model</a>. The service model union focuses more on maintaining worker rights, providing services, and resolving disputes. Alternately, the organizing model typically involves full-time <a href="/wiki/Union_organizer" title="Union organizer">union organizers</a>, who work by building up confidence, strong networks, and leaders within the workforce; and confrontational campaigns involving large numbers of union members. Many unions are a blend of these two philosophies, and the definitions of the models themselves are still debated.\n</p><p>In Britain, the perceived left-leaning nature of trade unions has resulted in the formation of a reactionary right-wing trade union called <a href="/wiki/Solidarity_%E2%80%93_The_Union_for_British_Workers" class="mw-redirect" title="Solidarity \xe2\x80\x93 The Union for British Workers">Solidarity</a> which is supported by the far-right <a href="/wiki/British_National_Party" title="British National Party">BNP</a>. In Denmark, there are some newer apolitical "discount" unions who offer a very basic level of services, as opposed to the dominating Danish pattern of extensive services and organizing.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg/220px-Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg/330px-Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg/440px-Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1760" data-file-height="1168" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A rally of the trade union <a href="/wiki/UNISON" class="mw-redirect" title="UNISON">UNISON</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a> during a strike on 28 March 2006</div></div></div>\n<p>In contrast, in several European countries (e.g. Belgium, Denmark, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>), religious unions have existed for decades. These unions typically distanced themselves from some of the doctrines of orthodox <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>, such as the preference of atheism and from rhetoric suggesting that employees\' interests always are in conflict with those of employers. Some of these Christian unions have had some ties to centrist or conservative political movements and some do not regard strikes as acceptable political means for achieving employees\' goals.<sup id="cite_ref-Poole,_M._1986_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poole,_M._1986-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, the biggest trade union <a href="/wiki/Solidarity_(Polish_trade_union)" title="Solidarity (Polish trade union)">Solidarity</a> emerged as an <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-communist</a> movement with <a href="/wiki/Religious_nationalism" title="Religious nationalism">religious nationalist</a> overtones<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> and today it supports the right-wing <a href="/wiki/Law_and_Justice" title="Law and Justice">Law and Justice</a> party.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although their political structure and autonomy varies widely, union leaderships are usually formed through democratic <a href="/wiki/Election" title="Election">elections</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Some research, such as that conducted by the Australian Centre for Industrial Relations Research and Training,<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> argues that unionized workers enjoy better conditions and wages than those who are not unionized.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Shop_types">Shop types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Shop types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Companies that employ workers with a union generally operate on one of several models:\n</p>\n<ul><li>A <a href="/wiki/Closed_shop" title="Closed shop">closed shop</a> (US) or a "pre-entry closed shop" (UK) employs only people who are already union members. The compulsory <a href="/wiki/Hiring_hall" title="Hiring hall">hiring hall</a> is an example of a closed shop \xe2\x80\x93 in this case the employer must recruit directly from the union, as well as the employee working strictly for unionized employers.</li>\n<li>A <a href="/wiki/Union_shop" title="Union shop">union shop</a> (US) or a "post-entry closed shop" (UK) employs non-union workers as well, but sets a time limit within which new employees must join a union.</li>\n<li>An <a href="/wiki/Agency_shop" title="Agency shop">agency shop</a> requires non-union workers to pay a fee to the union for its services in negotiating their contract. This is sometimes called the <a href="/wiki/Rand_formula" title="Rand formula">Rand formula</a>.</li>\n<li>An <a href="/wiki/Open_shop" title="Open shop">open shop</a> does not require union membership in employing or keeping workers. Where a union is active, workers who do not contribute to a union may include those who approve of the union contract (<a href="/wiki/Free_riders" class="mw-redirect" title="Free riders">free riders</a>) and those who do not. In the United States, state level <a href="/wiki/Right-to-work_law" title="Right-to-work law">right-to-work laws</a> mandate the open shop in some states. In Germany only open shops are legal; that is, all discrimination based on union membership is forbidden. This affects the function and services of the union.</li></ul>\n<p>An EU case concerning Italy stated that, "The principle of trade union freedom in the Italian system implies recognition of the right of the individual not to belong to any trade union ("negative" freedom of association/trade union freedom), and the unlawfulness of discrimination liable to cause harm to non-unionized employees."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Britain, previous to this EU jurisprudence, a series of laws introduced during the 1980s by Margaret Thatcher\'s government restricted closed and union shops. All agreements requiring a worker to join a union are now illegal. In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/Taft-Hartley_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Taft-Hartley Act">Taft-Hartley Act</a> of 1947 outlawed the closed shop.\n</p><p>In 2006, the European Court of Human Rights found Danish closed-shop agreements to be in breach of Article 11 of the European Convention on Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms. It was stressed that Denmark and <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> were among a limited number of contracting states that continue to permit the conclusion of closed-shop agreements.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversity_of_international_unions">Diversity of international unions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Diversity of international unions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Union law varies from country to country, as does the function of unions. For example, German and Dutch unions have played a greater role in management decisions through participation in corporate boards and <a href="/wiki/Co-determination" title="Co-determination">co-determination</a> than have unions in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, in the United States, collective bargaining is most commonly undertaken by unions directly with employers, whereas in Austria, Denmark, Germany or Sweden, unions most often negotiate with employers associations.\n</p><p>Concerning labour market regulation in the EU, Gold (1993)<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> and Hall (1994)<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> have identified three distinct systems of labour market regulation, which also influence the role that unions play:\n</p>\n<ul><li>"In the Continental European System of labour market regulation, the government plays an important role as there is a strong legislative core of employee rights, which provides the basis for agreements as well as a framework for discord between unions on one side and employers or employers\' associations on the other. This model was said to be found in EU core countries such as Belgium, France, Germany, the Netherlands and Italy, and it is also mirrored and emulated to some extent in the institutions of the EU, due to the relative weight that these countries had in the EU until the EU expansion by the inclusion of 10 new <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern European</a> member states in 2004.</li>\n<li>In the Anglo-Saxon System of labour market regulation, the government\'s legislative role is much more limited, which allows for more issues to be decided between employers and employees and any union or employers\' associations which might represent these parties in the decision-making process. However, in these countries, collective agreements are not widespread; only a few businesses and a few sectors of the economy have a strong tradition of finding collective solutions in labour relations. <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a> and the UK belong to this category, and in contrast to the EU core countries above, these countries first joined the EU in 1973.</li>\n<li>In the Nordic System of labour market regulation, the government\'s legislative role is limited in the same way as in the Anglo-Saxon system. However, in contrast to the countries in the Anglo-Saxon system category, this is a much more widespread network of collective agreements, which covers most industries and most firms. This model was said to encompass Denmark, Finland, Norway and Sweden. Here, Denmark joined the EU in 1973, whereas Finland and Sweden joined in 1995."<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>The United States takes a more <a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a> approach, setting some minimum standards but leaving most workers\' wages and benefits to collective bargaining and market forces. Thus, it comes closest to the above Anglo-Saxon model. Also, the Eastern European countries that have recently entered into the EU come closest to the Anglo-Saxon model.\n</p><p>In contrast, in Germany, the relation between individual employees and employers is considered to be asymmetrical. In consequence, many working conditions are not negotiable due to a strong legal protection of individuals. However, the German flavor or works legislation has as its main objective to create a balance of power between employees organized in unions and employers organized in employers associations. This allows much wider legal boundaries for collective bargaining, compared to the narrow boundaries for individual negotiations. As a condition to obtain the legal status of a trade union, employee associations need to prove that their leverage is strong enough to serve as a counterforce in negotiations with employers. If such an employees association is competing against another union, its leverage may be questioned by unions and then evaluated in a court trial. In Germany, only very few professional associations obtained the right to negotiate salaries and working conditions for their members, notably the medical doctors association Marburger Bund and the pilots association Vereinigung Cockpit. The engineers association <a href="/wiki/Verein_Deutscher_Ingenieure" title="Verein Deutscher Ingenieure">Verein Deutscher Ingenieure</a> does not strive to act as a union, as it also represents the interests of engineering businesses.\n</p><p>Beyond the classification listed above, unions\' relations with political parties vary. In many countries unions are tightly bonded, or even share leadership, with a political party intended to represent the interests of the working class. Typically this is a <a href="/wiki/Left-wing" class="mw-redirect" title="Left-wing">left-wing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Socialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialist">socialist</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Social_democrat" class="mw-redirect" title="Social democrat">social democratic</a> party, but many exceptions exist, including some of the aforementioned Christian unions.<sup id="cite_ref-Poole,_M._1986_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poole,_M._1986-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In the United States, trade unions are almost always aligned with the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> with a few exceptions. For example, the <a href="/wiki/International_Brotherhood_of_Teamsters" title="International Brotherhood of Teamsters">International Brotherhood of Teamsters</a> has supported <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a> candidates on a number of occasions and the <a href="/wiki/Professional_Air_Traffic_Controllers_Organization_(1968)" title="Professional Air Traffic Controllers Organization (1968)">Professional Air Traffic Controllers Organization</a> (PATCO) endorsed <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> in 1980. In Britain trade union movement\'s relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> frayed as party leadership embarked on <a href="/wiki/Privatization" title="Privatization">privatization</a> plans at odds with what unions see as the worker\'s interests. However, it has strengthened once more after the Labour party\'s election of <a href="/wiki/Ed_Miliband" title="Ed Miliband">Ed Miliband</a>, who beat his brother <a href="/wiki/David_Miliband" title="David Miliband">David Miliband</a> to become leader of the party after Ed secured the trade union votes. Additionally, in the past, there was a group known as the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Trade_Unionists" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative Trade Unionists">Conservative Trade Unionists</a>, or CTU, formed of people who sympathized with right wing Tory policy but were Trade Unionists.\n</p><p>Historically, the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Korea" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Korea">Republic of Korea</a> has regulated collective bargaining by requiring employers to participate, but collective bargaining has only been legal if held in sessions before the <a href="/wiki/Korean_New_Year" title="Korean New Year">lunar new year</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_unionization">International unionization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: International unionization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The largest trade union federation in the world is the <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>-based <a href="/wiki/International_Trade_Union_Confederation" title="International Trade Union Confederation">International Trade Union Confederation</a> (ITUC), which has approximately 309 affiliated organizations in 156 countries and territories, with a combined membership of 166&#160;million. The ITUC is a federation of national trade union centres, such as the AFL-CIO in the United States and the Trades Union Congress in the United Kingdom. Other global trade union organizations include the <a href="/wiki/World_Federation_of_Trade_Unions" title="World Federation of Trade Unions">World Federation of Trade Unions</a>.\n</p><p>National and regional trade unions organizing in specific industry sectors or occupational groups also form <a href="/wiki/Global_union_federation" title="Global union federation">global union federations</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Union_Network_International" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Network International">Union Network International</a>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Transport_Workers_Federation" class="mw-redirect" title="International Transport Workers Federation">International Transport Workers Federation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_Journalists" title="International Federation of Journalists">International Federation of Journalists</a>, the International Arts and Entertainment Alliance or <a href="/wiki/Public_Services_International" title="Public Services International">Public Services International</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact">Impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The academic literature shows substantial evidence that labor unions reduce <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">economic inequality</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The economist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stiglitz" title="Joseph Stiglitz">Joseph Stiglitz</a> has asserted that, "Strong unions have helped to reduce inequality, whereas weaker unions have made it easier for <a href="/wiki/CEO" class="mw-redirect" title="CEO">CEOs</a>, sometimes working with market forces that they have helped shape, to increase it." The decline in unionization since the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> in the United States has been associated with a pronounced rise in <a href="/wiki/Income_inequality_in_the_United_States" title="Income inequality in the United States">income</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wealth_inequality_in_the_United_States" title="Wealth inequality in the United States">wealth inequality</a> and, since 1967, with loss of <a href="/wiki/American_middle_class" title="American middle class">middle class</a> income.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n<a href="/wiki/Right-to-work_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Right-to-work laws">Right-to-work laws</a> have been linked to greater economic inequality in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Research from Norway has found that high unionization rates lead to substantial increases in firm productivity, as well as increases in workers\' wages.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Research from Belgium also found productivity gains, although smaller.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Other research in the United States finds that unions can harm profitability, employment and business growth rates.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Research from the <a href="/wiki/Anglosphere" title="Anglosphere">Anglosphere</a> indicates that unions can provide wage premiums and reduce inequality while reducing employment growth and restricting employment flexibility.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Milton_Friedman" title="Milton Friedman">Milton Friedman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economist" title="Economist">economist</a> and advocate of <a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire_capitalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Laissez-faire capitalism">laissez-faire capitalism</a>, sought to show that unionization produces higher wages (for the union members) at the expense of fewer jobs, and that, if some industries are unionized while others are not, wages will tend to decline in non-unionized industries.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the United States, the weakening of unions has been linked to more favorable electoral outcomes for the Republican Party.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Legislators in areas with high unionization rates are more responsive to the interests of the poor, whereas areas with lower unionization rates are more responsive to the interests of the rich.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Higher unionization rates increase the likelihood of parental leave policies being adopted.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Republican-controlled states are less likely to adopt more restrictive labor policies when unions are strong in the state.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Research in the United States found that American congressional representatives were more responsive to the interests of the poor in districts with higher unionization rates.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Another 2020 American study found an association between US state level adoption of parental leave legislation and labor union strength.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the United States, unions have been linked to lower racial resentment among whites.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Membership in unions increases political knowledge, in particular among those with less formal education.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the United States, higher union density has been associated with lower suicide/overdose deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_publications">Union publications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Union publications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Several sources of current news exist about the trade union movement in the world. These include <a href="/wiki/LabourStart" title="LabourStart">LabourStart</a> and the official website of the international trade union movement <a href="/wiki/Global_Unions" title="Global Unions">Global Unions</a>. A source of international news about unions is RadioLabour which provides daily (Monday to Friday) news reports.\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Labor_Notes" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor Notes">Labor Notes</a></i> is the largest circulation cross-union publication remaining in the United States. It reports news and analysis about union activity or problems facing the labour movement. Another source of union news is the <i>Workers Independent News</i>, a news organization providing radio articles to independent and syndicated radio shows in the United States.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Film">Film</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Film">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="box-In_popular_culture plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>appears to contain <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not#Wikipedia_is_not_an_indiscriminate_collection_of_information" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not">trivial, minor, or unrelated</a> references to <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Trivia_sections#&quot;In_popular_culture&quot;_and_&quot;Cultural_references&quot;_material" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Trivia sections">popular culture</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please reorganize this content to explain the subject\'s impact on popular culture, <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">providing citations</a> to <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research">reliable, secondary sources</a>, rather than simply listing appearances. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2020</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<ul><li>The 2010 British film <i><a href="/wiki/Made_in_Dagenham" title="Made in Dagenham">Made in Dagenham</a></i>, starring <a href="/wiki/Sally_Hawkins" title="Sally Hawkins">Sally Hawkins</a>, dramatizes the <a href="/wiki/Ford_sewing_machinists_strike_of_1968" title="Ford sewing machinists strike of 1968">Ford sewing machinists strike of 1968</a> that aimed for equal pay for women.</li>\n<li>Trade unions were often portrayed in the scripts of <a href="/wiki/Jim_Allen_(playwright)" title="Jim Allen (playwright)">Jim Allen</a>. Examples include <i><a href="/wiki/The_Big_Flame" title="The Big Flame">The Big Flame</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rank_and_File" title="The Rank and File">The Rank and File</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Days_of_Hope" title="Days of Hope">Days of Hope</a></i>. These films all depict union leaders as untrustworthy and prone to betraying the striking workers.</li>\n<li>The British <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Mineworkers_(Great_Britain)" title="National Union of Mineworkers (Great Britain)">National Union of Mineworkers</a> has been portrayed in numerous films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Brassed_Off" title="Brassed Off">Brassed Off</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Billy_Elliot" title="Billy Elliot">Billy Elliot</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Pride_(2014_film)" title="Pride (2014 film)">Pride</a></i>.</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Bastard_Boys" title="Bastard Boys">Bastard Boys</a></i>, a 2007 dramatization of the <a href="/wiki/1998_Australian_waterfront_dispute" title="1998 Australian waterfront dispute">1998 Australian waterfront dispute</a>.</li>\n<li>The 2000 film <a href="/wiki/Bread_and_Roses_(2000_film)" title="Bread and Roses (2000 film)"><i>Bread and Roses</i></a> deals with the struggle of poorly paid janitorial workers in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> and their fight for better working conditions and the right to unionize.</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Hoffa_(film)" title="Hoffa (film)">Hoffa</a></i>, a 1992 American <a href="/wiki/Biographical_film" title="Biographical film">biographical film</a> directed by <a href="/wiki/Danny_DeVito" title="Danny DeVito">Danny DeVito</a> and based on the life of <a href="/wiki/Teamsters_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Teamsters Union">Teamsters Union</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Hoffa" title="Jimmy Hoffa">Jimmy Hoffa</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Matewan" title="Matewan">Matewan</a> is a 1987 American drama film written and directed by <a href="/wiki/John_Sayles" title="John Sayles">John Sayles</a> that dramatizes the events of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Matewan" title="Battle of Matewan">Battle of Matewan</a>, a coal miners\' strike in 1920 in Matewan, a small town in the hills of West Virginia. <a href="/wiki/Haskell_Wexler" title="Haskell Wexler">Haskell Wexler</a> was nominated for the Academy Award for Best Cinematography.</li>\n<li>The 1985 documentary film <i><a href="/wiki/Final_Offer" title="Final Offer">Final Offer</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Sturla_Gunnarsson" title="Sturla Gunnarsson">Sturla Gunnarsson</a> and Robert Collision shows the 1984 union contract negotiations with <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>.</li>\n<li>The 1979 film <i><a href="/wiki/Norma_Rae" title="Norma Rae">Norma Rae</a></i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Ritt" title="Martin Ritt">Martin Ritt</a> and starring <a href="/wiki/Sally_Field" title="Sally Field">Sally Field</a>, is based on the true story of <a href="/wiki/Crystal_Lee_Sutton" title="Crystal Lee Sutton">Crystal Lee Jordan</a>\'s successful attempt to unionize her textile factory.</li>\n<li>The 1978 film <i><a href="/wiki/F.I.S.T." title="F.I.S.T.">F.I.S.T.</a></i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Norman_Jewison" title="Norman Jewison">Norman Jewison</a> and starring <a href="/wiki/Sylvester_Stallone" title="Sylvester Stallone">Sylvester Stallone</a>, is loosely based on the Teamsters Union and their former President Jimmy Hoffa.</li>\n<li>The 1959 film <i><a href="/wiki/I%27m_All_Right_Jack" title="I&#39;m All Right Jack">I\'m All Right Jack</a></i>, a comedy with <a href="/wiki/Peter_Sellers" title="Peter Sellers">Peter Sellers</a> playing the <a href="/wiki/Shop_steward" title="Shop steward">shop steward</a> Fred Kite.</li>\n<li>The 1954 film <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Waterfront" title="On the Waterfront">On the Waterfront</a></i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Elia_Kazan" title="Elia Kazan">Elia Kazan</a>, concerns union violence among longshoremen.</li>\n<li>Other documentaries: <i><a href="/wiki/Made_in_L.A._(2007_film)" title="Made in L.A. (2007 film)">Made in L.A.</a></i> (2007); <i><a href="/wiki/American_Standoff" title="American Standoff">American Standoff</a></i> (2002); <i>The Fight in the Fields</i> (1997); <i><a cs1">Eisenberg\xe2\x80\x90Guyot, Jerzy; Mooney, Stephen J.; Hagopian, Amy; Barrington, Wendy E.; Hajat, Anjum (2020). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC7293351">"Solidarity and disparity: Declining labor union density and changing racial and educational mortality inequities in the United States"</a>. <i>American Journal of Industrial Medicine</i>. <b>63</b> (3): 218\xe2\x80\x93231. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1002%2Fajim.23081">10.1002/ajim.23081</a>. <a href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/1097-0274">1097-0274</a>. <a href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a>&#160;<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC7293351">7293351</a></span>. <a href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/31845387">31845387</a>. <q>Results - Overall, a 10% increase in union density was associated with a 17% relative decrease in overdose/suicide mortality (95% confidence interval [CI]: 0.70, 0.98), or 5.7 lives saved per 100\xe2\x80\x89000 person\xe2\x80\x90years (95% CI: \xe2\x88\x9210.7, \xe2\x88\x920.7). Union density\'s absolute (lives\xe2\x80\x90saved) effects on overdose/suicide mortality were stronger for men than women, but its relative effects were similar across genders. Union density had little effect on all\xe2\x80\x90cause mortality overall or across subgroups, and modeling suggested union\xe2\x80\x90density increases would not affect mortality inequities. Conclusions - Declining union density (as operationalized in this study) may not explain all\xe2\x80\x90cause mortality inequities, although increases in union density may reduce overdose/suicide mortality.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=American+Journal+of+Industrial+Medicine&amp;rft.atitle=Solidarity+and+disparity%3A+Declining+labor+union+density+and+changing+racial+and+educational+mortality+inequities+in+the+United+States&amp;rft.volume=63&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=218-231&amp;rft.date=2020&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC7293351%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft.issn=1097-0274&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F31845387&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1002%2Fajim.23081&amp;rft.aulast=Eisenberg%E2%80%90Guyot&amp;rft.aufirst=Jerzy&amp;rft.au=Mooney%2C+Stephen+J.&amp;rft.au=Hagopian%2C+Amy&amp;rft.au=Barrington%2C+Wendy+E.&amp;rft.au=Hajat%2C+Anjum&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC7293351&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATrade+union" class="Z3988"></span></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Britain">Britain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Britain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>Aldcroft, D.
